<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Viscosity</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;font-style:italic;">Viscosity</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Viscosities.gif" title="Viscosities"><img alt="Viscosities" data-file-height="463" data-file-width="600" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Viscosities.gif/300px-Viscosities.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Viscosities.gif/450px-Viscosities.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/44/Viscosities.gif 2x" width="300"/></a><div>A simulation of liquids with different viscosities. The liquid on the right has higher viscosity than the liquid on the left.</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">Common symbols</div></th><td>
<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eta_(letter)" title="Eta (letter)">η</a></span>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;"><a href="/wiki/Mu_(letter)" title="Mu (letter)">μ</a></span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">Derivations from<br/>other quantities</div></th><td>
<span class="texhtml"><i>μ</i> = <i><a href="/wiki/Shear_modulus" title="Shear modulus">G</a></i>·<i><a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">t</a></i></span></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>viscosity</b> of a <a href="/wiki/Fluid" title="Fluid">fluid</a> is the measure of its <a href="/wiki/Drag_(physics)" title="Drag (physics)">resistance</a> to gradual deformation by <a href="/wiki/Shear_stress" title="Shear stress">shear stress</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tensile_stress" title="Tensile stress">tensile stress</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> For liquids, it corresponds to the informal concept of "thickness": for example, <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a> has a higher viscosity than <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Viscosity is the property of a fluid which opposes the relative motion between two surfaces of the fluid that are moving at different <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocities</a>. In simple terms, viscosity means friction between the molecules of fluid. When the fluid is forced through a tube, the particles which compose the fluid generally move more quickly near the tube's axis and more slowly near its walls; therefore some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stress_(physics)" title="Stress (physics)">stress</a> (such as a <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a> difference between the two ends of the tube) is needed to overcome the <a href="/wiki/Friction" title="Friction">friction</a> between particle layers to keep the fluid moving. For a given velocity pattern, the stress required is proportional to the fluid's viscosity.
</p><p>A fluid that has no resistance to shear stress is known as an <i>ideal</i> or <i>inviscid</i> fluid. Zero viscosity is observed only at <a href="/wiki/Cryogenics" title="Cryogenics">very low temperatures</a> in <a href="/wiki/Superfluidity" title="Superfluidity">superfluids</a>. Otherwise, all fluids have positive viscosity and are technically said to be viscous or viscid. A fluid with a relatively high viscosity, such as <a href="/wiki/Pitch_(resin)" title="Pitch (resin)">pitch</a>, may appear to be a <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">solid</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Viscosity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The word "viscosity" is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> "<i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">viscum</i></i>", meaning <a href="/wiki/Mistletoe" title="Mistletoe">mistletoe</a> and also a viscous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glue" title="Glue">glue</a> made from mistletoe berries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EtymOnline_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-EtymOnline-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Viscosity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Simple_definition">Simple definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Viscosity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Simple definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Laminar_shear.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="250" data-file-width="350" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Laminar_shear.svg/250px-Laminar_shear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Laminar_shear.svg/375px-Laminar_shear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Laminar_shear.svg/500px-Laminar_shear.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Laminar_shear.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of a planar Couette flow. Since the shearing flow is opposed by friction between adjacent layers of fluid (which are in relative motion), a force is required to sustain the motion of the upper plate. The relative strength of this force is a measure of the fluid's viscosity.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Laminar_shear_flow.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="250" data-file-width="250" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Laminar_shear_flow.svg/250px-Laminar_shear_flow.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Laminar_shear_flow.svg/375px-Laminar_shear_flow.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Laminar_shear_flow.svg/500px-Laminar_shear_flow.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Laminar_shear_flow.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In a general parallel flow, the shear stress is proportional to the gradient of the velocity.</div></div></div>
<p>The viscosity of a fluid expresses its resistance to shearing flows, in which adjacent fluid layers are in relative motion. A simple example of such a shearing flow is a planar <a href="/wiki/Couette_flow" title="Couette flow">Couette flow</a>, where a fluid is trapped between two infinitely large plates, one fixed and one in parallel motion at constant speed <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle u}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>u</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle u}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="u" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c3e6bb763d22c20916ed4f0bb6bd49d7470cffd8" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.33ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> (see illustration to the right). Although viscosity applies to general flows, it is easy to define and visualize in a Couette flow.
</p><p>If the speed of the top plate is low enough, then in steady state the fluid particles move parallel to it, and their speed varies from <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle 0}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mn>0</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle 0}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle 0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2aae8864a3c1fec9585261791a809ddec1489950" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.162ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> at the bottom to <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">u</span> at the top. Each layer of fluid moves faster than the one just below it, and friction between them gives rise to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Force_(physics)" title="Force (physics)">force</a> resisting their relative motion. In particular, the fluid applies on the top plate a force in the direction opposite to its motion, and an equal but opposite force on the bottom plate. An external force is therefore required in order to keep the top plate moving at constant speed.
</p><p>In many fluids, the flow velocity is observed to vary linearly from zero at the bottom to <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle u}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>u</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle u}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="u" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c3e6bb763d22c20916ed4f0bb6bd49d7470cffd8" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.33ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> at the top. Moreover, the magnitude <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">F</span> of the force acting on the top plate is found to be proportional to the speed <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">u</span> and the area <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">A</span> of each plate, and inversely proportional to their separation <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">y</span>: 
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F=\mu A{\frac {u}{y}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>F</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>μ<!-- μ --></mi>
<mi>A</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>u</mi>
<mi>y</mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle F=\mu A{\frac {u}{y}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="F=\mu A{\frac {u}{y}}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/413ef0745d061f367dd3cb768a833a47c5e5bae4" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:10.797ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>The proportionality factor <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">μ</span> is the viscosity of the fluid, with units of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\text{Pa}}\cdot {\text{s}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>Pa</mtext>
</mrow>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>s</mtext>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\text{Pa}}\cdot {\text{s}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\text{Pa}}\cdot {\text{s}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1dc60d05bebc0154c784a366bf7f404a1e5d6dd1" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:5.341ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> (<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">pascal</a>-<a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">second</a>). The ratio <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;"><span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">u</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">y</span></span></span> is called the <i>rate of shear deformation</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Shear_velocity" title="Shear velocity">shear velocity</a></i>, and is the <a href="/wiki/Derivative" title="Derivative">derivative</a> of the fluid speed in the direction <a href="/wiki/Perpendicular" title="Perpendicular">perpendicular</a> to the plates (see illustrations to the right). If the velocity does not vary linearly with <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">y</span>, then the appropriate generalization is
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \tau =\mu {\frac {\partial u}{\partial y}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mo>=</mo>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>

<mi>u</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow>

<mi>y</mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \tau =\mu {\frac {\partial u}{\partial y}},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\tau =\mu {\frac {\partial u}{\partial y}}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/36db791abdbffb5b7822b4d2ecda9706c70f4777" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:9.833ex; height:6.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>where <span class="texhtml"><i>τ</i> = <i><span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">F</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">A</span></span></i></span>, and <span class="texhtml"><span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">∂<i>u</i></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">∂<i>y</i></span></span></span> is the local shear velocity. This expression is referred to as Newton's law of viscosity. In shearing flows with planar symmetry, it is what <i>defines</i> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<mi>μ<!-- μ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mu }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mu " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9fd47b2a39f7a7856952afec1f1db72c67af6161" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:1.402ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>. It is a special case of the general definition of viscosity (see below), which can be expressed in coordinate-free form.
</p><p>Use of the <a href="/wiki/Mu_(letter)" title="Mu (letter)">Greek letter mu</a> (<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">μ</span>) for the viscosity is common among mechanical and chemical engineers, as well as physicists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> However, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eta_(letter)" title="Eta (letter)">Greek letter eta</a> (<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">η</span>) is also used by chemists, physicists, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IUPAC" title="IUPAC">IUPAC</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The viscosity <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>μ<!-- μ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mu }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mu " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9fd47b2a39f7a7856952afec1f1db72c67af6161" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:1.402ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is sometimes also referred to as the <i>shear viscosity</i>. However, at least one author discourages the use of this terminology, noting that <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mu }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mu " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9fd47b2a39f7a7856952afec1f1db72c67af6161" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:1.402ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> can be appear in nonshearing flows in addition to shearing flows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_definition">General definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Viscosity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: General definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Viscous_stress_tensor" title="Viscous stress tensor">Viscous stress tensor</a></div>
<p>In general, the <a href="/wiki/Stress_(mechanics)" title="Stress (mechanics)">stresses</a> within a flow can be attributed partly to the <a href="/wiki/Deformation_(mechanics)" title="Deformation (mechanics)">deformation</a> of the material from some rest state (<a href="/wiki/Elasticity_(physics)" title="Elasticity (physics)">elastic</a> stress), and partly to the <a href="/wiki/Strain_rate" title="Strain rate">rate of change of the deformation</a> over time (viscous stress). In a fluid, by definition, the elastic stress includes only the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_pressure" title="Hydrostatic pressure">hydrostatic pressure</a>. In very general terms, the fluid's viscosity is the relation between the strain rate and the viscous stress. In the <a href="/wiki/Newtonian_fluid" title="Newtonian fluid">Newtonian fluid</a> model the relationship is by definition a linear map, described by a viscosity tensor that, when multiplied by the <a href="/wiki/Strain_rate_tensor" title="Strain rate tensor">strain rate tensor</a> (which is the <a href="/wiki/Gradient" title="Gradient">gradient</a> of the flow's velocity), gives the viscous stress tensor. In Cartesian coordinates, this gives<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \tau _{ij}=\sum _{k}\sum _{l}\mu _{ijkl}{\frac {\partial v_{k}}{\partial r_{l}}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>
</munder>

<mo>∑<!-- ∑ --></mo>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \tau _{ij}=\sum _{k}\sum _{l}\mu _{ijkl}{\frac {\partial v_{k}}{\partial r_{l}}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \tau _{ij}=\sum _{k}\sum _{l}\mu _{ijkl}{\frac {\partial v_{k}}{\partial r_{l}}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8f1b26cd7d00520673f27b8311effe65365a43c8" style="vertical-align: -3.005ex; width:22.319ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>Since the indices in the above expression can vary from 1 to 3, there are 81 "viscosity coefficients" <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mu _{ijkl}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>i</mi>
<mi>j</mi>
<mi>k</mi>
<mi>l</mi>

</msub>

</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \mu _{ijkl}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0ccc7586bc977f4d32cd07ebe15e44c6bd85a983" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:4.226ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span> in total. However, due to spatial symmetries these coefficients are not all independent. For instance, for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotropic" title="Isotropic">isotropic</a> Newtonian fluids, the 81 coefficients can be reduced to 2 independent parameters. The most usual decomposition yields the standard (scalar) viscosity <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">μ</span> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bulk_viscosity" title="Bulk viscosity">bulk viscosity</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \kappa }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {\delta } }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

This equation can be thought of as a generalized form of Newton's law of viscosity. 
</p><p>The bulk viscosity expresses a type of internal friction that resists the shearless compression or expansion of a fluid. Knowledge of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \kappa }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>κ<!-- κ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \kappa }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\kappa " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/54ddec2e922c5caea4e47d04feef86e782dc8e6d" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.339ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> is frequently not necessary in fluid dynamics problems. For example, incompressible liquids satisfy <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \nabla \cdot \mathbf {v} =0}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi mathvariant="normal">∇<!-- ∇ --></mi>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">v</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>0</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \nabla \cdot \mathbf {v} =0}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\nabla \cdot {\mathbf  {v}}=0" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/760025de0202db29f86a1c2c4bef18d54ccc0f64" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:9.287ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> and so the term containing <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \kappa }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

</mstyle>
</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\kappa " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/54ddec2e922c5caea4e47d04feef86e782dc8e6d" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.339ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> is absent. Moreover, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \kappa }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>κ<!-- κ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \kappa }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\kappa " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/54ddec2e922c5caea4e47d04feef86e782dc8e6d" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.339ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> is often assumed to be negligible for gases since it is <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle 0}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mn>0</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle 0}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle 0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2aae8864a3c1fec9585261791a809ddec1489950" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.162ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monoatomic" title="Monoatomic">monoatomic</a> <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas" title="Ideal gas">ideal gas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bird2007_p19_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bird2007_p19-10">[10]</a></sup> One situation in which <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \kappa }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \kappa }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\kappa " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/54ddec2e922c5caea4e47d04feef86e782dc8e6d" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.339ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> can be important is the calculation of energy loss in <a href="/wiki/Sound" title="Sound">sound</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shock_wave" title="Shock wave">shock waves</a>, described by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stokes%27_law_(sound_attenuation)" title="Stokes' law (sound attenuation)">Stokes' law of sound attenuation</a>, since these phenomena involve rapid expansions and compressions. 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dynamic_and_kinematic_viscosity">Dynamic and kinematic viscosity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Viscosity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Dynamic and kinematic viscosity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In fluid dynamics, it is common to work in terms of the <i>kinematic viscosity</i> (also called "momentum diffusivity"), defined as the ratio of the viscosity <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">μ</span> to the <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> of the fluid <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">ρ</span>. It is usually denoted by the <a href="/wiki/Nu_(letter)" title="Nu (letter)">Greek letter nu</a> (<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">ν</span>) and has units <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathrm {(length)^{2}/time} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \nu ={\frac {\mu }{\rho }}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>Consistent with this nomenclature, the viscosity <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mu " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9fd47b2a39f7a7856952afec1f1db72c67af6161" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:1.402ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is frequently called the <i>dynamic viscosity</i>. 
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Newtonian_and_non-Newtonian_fluids">Newtonian and non-Newtonian fluids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Viscosity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Newtonian and non-Newtonian fluids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Viscous_regimes_chart.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="835" data-file-width="821" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Viscous_regimes_chart.png/250px-Viscous_regimes_chart.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Viscous_regimes_chart.png/375px-Viscous_regimes_chart.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Viscous_regimes_chart.png/500px-Viscous_regimes_chart.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Viscous_regimes_chart.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Viscosity, the slope of each line, varies among materials.</div></div></div>
<p>Newton's law of viscosity is a <a href="/wiki/Constitutive_equation" title="Constitutive equation">constitutive equation</a> (like <a href="/wiki/Hooke%27s_law" title="Hooke's law">Hooke's law</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fick%27s_law" title="Fick's law">Fick's law</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ohm%27s_law" title="Ohm's law">Ohm's law</a>): it is not a fundamental law of nature but an approximation that holds in some materials and fails in others.
</p><p>A fluid that behaves according to Newton's law, with a viscosity <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">μ</span> that is independent of the stress, is said to be <a href="/wiki/Newtonian_fluid" title="Newtonian fluid">Newtonian</a>. <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">Gases</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, and many common liquids can be considered Newtonian in ordinary conditions and contexts. There are many <a href="/wiki/Non-Newtonian_fluid" title="Non-Newtonian fluid">non-Newtonian fluids</a> that significantly deviate from that law in some way or other. For example:
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shear_thickening" title="Shear thickening">Shear-thickening</a> liquids, whose viscosity increases with the rate of shear strain.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Shear_thinning" title="Shear thinning">Shear-thinning</a> liquids, whose viscosity decreases with the rate of shear strain.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thixotropic" title="Thixotropic">Thixotropic</a> liquids, that become less viscous over time when shaken, agitated, or otherwise stressed.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rheopectic" title="Rheopectic">Rheopectic</a> (dilatant) liquids, that become more viscous over time when shaken, agitated, or otherwise stressed.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bingham_plastic" title="Bingham plastic">Bingham plastics</a> that behave as a solid at low stresses but flow as a viscous fluid at high stresses.</li></ul>
<p>Shear-thinning liquids are very commonly, but misleadingly, described as thixotropic.
</p><p>Even for a Newtonian fluid, the viscosity usually depends on its composition and temperature. For gases and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compressible_fluid" title="Compressible fluid">compressible fluids</a>, it depends on temperature and varies very slowly with pressure.
</p><p>The viscosity of some fluids may depend on other factors. A <a href="/wiki/Magnetorheological_fluid" title="Magnetorheological fluid">magnetorheological fluid</a>, for example, becomes thicker when subjected to a <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a>, possibly to the point of behaving like a solid.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_solids">In solids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Viscosity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: In solids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The viscous forces that arise during fluid flow must not be confused with the <a href="/wiki/Elasticity_(physics)" title="Elasticity (physics)">elastic</a> forces that arise in a solid in response to shear, compression or extension stresses. While in the latter the stress is proportional to the <i>amount</i> of shear deformation, in a fluid it is proportional to the <i>rate</i> of deformation over time. (For this reason, <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">Maxwell</a> used the term <i>fugitive elasticity</i> for fluid viscosity.)
</p><p>However, many liquids (including water) will briefly react like elastic solids when subjected to sudden stress. Conversely, many "solids" (even <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a>) will flow like liquids, albeit very slowly, even under arbitrarily small stress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kumagai_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kumagai-11">[11]</a></sup> Such materials are therefore best described as possessing both elasticity (reaction to deformation) and viscosity (reaction to rate of deformation); that is, being <a href="/wiki/Viscoelasticity" title="Viscoelasticity">viscoelastic</a>.
</p><p>Indeed, some authors have claimed that <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_solid" title="Amorphous solid">amorphous solids</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a> and many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polymers" title="Polymers">polymers</a>, are actually liquids with a very high viscosity (greater than 10<sup>12</sup> Pa·s).
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elert_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elert-12">[12]</a></sup> However, other authors dispute this hypothesis, claiming instead that there is some threshold for the stress, below which most solids will not flow at all,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> and that alleged instances of glass flow in window panes of old buildings are due to the crude manufacturing process of older eras rather than to the viscosity of glass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Viscoelastic solids may exhibit both shear viscosity and bulk viscosity. The <a href="/wiki/Extensional_viscosity" title="Extensional viscosity">extensional viscosity</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Linear_combination" title="Linear combination">linear combination</a> of the shear and bulk viscosities that describes the reaction of a solid elastic material to elongation. It is widely used for characterizing polymers.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a>, earth materials that exhibit viscous deformation at least three <a href="/wiki/Order_of_magnitude" title="Order of magnitude">orders of magnitude</a> greater than their elastic deformation are sometimes called <a href="/wiki/Rheid" title="Rheid">rheids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Measurement">Measurement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Viscosity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Measurement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Viscometer" title="Viscometer">Viscometer</a></div>
<p>Viscosity is measured with various types of <a href="/wiki/Viscometer" title="Viscometer">viscometers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rheometer" title="Rheometer">rheometers</a>. A rheometer is used for those fluids that cannot be defined by a single value of viscosity and therefore require more parameters to be set and measured than is the case for a viscometer. Close temperature control of the fluid is essential to acquire accurate measurements, particularly in materials like lubricants, whose viscosity can double with a change of only 5 °C.
</p><p>For some fluids, the viscosity is constant over a wide range of shear rates (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newtonian_fluids" title="Newtonian fluids">Newtonian fluids</a>). The fluids without a constant viscosity (<a href="/wiki/Non-Newtonian_fluid" title="Non-Newtonian fluid">non-Newtonian fluids</a>) cannot be described by a single number. Non-Newtonian fluids exhibit a variety of different correlations between shear stress and shear rate.
</p><p>One of the most common instruments for measuring kinematic viscosity is the glass capillary viscometer.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Coating" title="Coating">coating</a> industries, viscosity may be measured with a cup in which the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Efflux_time" title="Efflux time">efflux time</a> is measured. There are several sorts of cup – such as the <a href="/wiki/Zahn_cup" title="Zahn cup">Zahn cup</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ford_viscosity_cup" title="Ford viscosity cup">Ford viscosity cup</a> – with the usage of each type varying mainly according to the industry. The efflux time can also be converted to kinematic viscosities (centistokes, cSt) through the conversion equations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Also used in coatings, a Stormer viscometer uses load-based rotation in order to determine viscosity. The viscosity is reported in Krebs units (KU), which are unique to Stormer viscometers.
</p><p>Vibrating viscometers can also be used to measure viscosity. Resonant, or vibrational viscometers work by creating shear waves within the liquid. In this method, the sensor is submerged in the fluid and is made to resonate at a specific frequency. As the surface of the sensor shears through the liquid, energy is lost due to its viscosity. This dissipated energy is then measured and converted into a viscosity reading. A higher viscosity causes a greater loss of energy.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><i>Extensional viscosity</i> can be measured with various <a href="/wiki/Rheometer" title="Rheometer">rheometers</a> that apply <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extensional_stress" title="Extensional stress">extensional stress</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Volume_viscosity" title="Volume viscosity">Volume viscosity</a> can be measured with an <a href="/wiki/Acoustic_rheometer" title="Acoustic rheometer">acoustic rheometer</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Apparent_viscosity" title="Apparent viscosity">Apparent viscosity</a> is a calculation derived from tests performed on <a href="/wiki/Drilling_fluid" title="Drilling fluid">drilling fluid</a> used in oil or gas well development. These calculations and tests help engineers develop and maintain the properties of the drilling fluid to the specifications required.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Units">Units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Viscosity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Dynamic_viscosity.2C_.CE.BC"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Dynamic_viscosity,_μ">Dynamic viscosity, <i><span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">μ</span></i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Viscosity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Dynamic viscosity, μ">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The SI unit of dynamic viscosity is Pa·s or kg·m<sup>−1</sup>·s<sup>−1</sup>
</p><p>Both the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_unit" title="Physical unit">physical unit</a> of dynamic viscosity in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SI" title="SI">SI</a> units, the <a href="/wiki/Poiseuille" title="Poiseuille">poiseuille</a> (Pl), and cgs units, the <a href="/wiki/Poise_(unit)" title="Poise (unit)">poise</a> (P), are named after <a href="/wiki/Jean_L%C3%A9onard_Marie_Poiseuille" title="Jean Léonard Marie Poiseuille">Jean Léonard Marie Poiseuille</a>. The poiseuille, which is rarely used, is equivalent to the <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">pascal</a> <a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">second</a> (Pa·s), or (N·s)/m<sup>2</sup>, or kg/(m·s). If a <a href="/wiki/Fluid" title="Fluid">fluid</a> is placed between two plates with distance one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meter" title="Meter">meter</a>, and one plate is pushed sideways with a <a href="/wiki/Shear_stress" title="Shear stress">shear stress</a> of one <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">pascal</a>, and it moves at <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">x</span> meters per <a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">second</a>, then it has viscosity of <span class="texhtml"><span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>x</i></span></span></span> pascal seconds. For example, water at 20 °C has a viscosity of 1.002 mPa·s, while a typical motor oil could have a viscosity of about 250 mPa·s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The units used in practice are either Pa·s and its submultiples or the cgs poise referred to below, and its submultiples.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cgs" title="Cgs">cgs</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_unit" title="Physical unit">physical unit</a> for dynamic viscosity, the poise<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> (P), is also named after Jean Poiseuille. It is more commonly expressed, particularly in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ASTM" title="ASTM">ASTM</a> standards, as <i>centipoise</i> (cP) since the latter is equal to the SI multiple millipascal seconds (mPa·s). For example, water at 20 °C has a viscosity of 1.002 mPa·s = 1.002 cP.
</p>
<dl><dd>1 Pl = 1 Pa·s</dd>
<dd>1 P = 1 dPa·s = 0.1 Pa·s = 0.1 kg·m<sup>−1</sup>·s<sup>−1</sup></dd>
<dd>1 cP = 1 mPa·s = 0.001 Pa·s = 0.001 N·s·m<sup>−2</sup> = 0.001 kg·m<sup>−1</sup>·s<sup>−1</sup>.</dd></dl>
<h3><span id="Kinematic_viscosity.2C_.CE.BD"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Kinematic_viscosity,_ν"><span id="Stokes_(unit)"></span>Kinematic viscosity, <i><span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">ν</span></i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Viscosity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Kinematic viscosity, ν">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The SI unit of kinematic viscosity is m<sup>2</sup>/s.
</p><p>The cgs physical unit for kinematic viscosity is the <i>stokes</i> (St), named after Sir <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Gabriel_Stokes" title="George Gabriel Stokes">George Gabriel Stokes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> It is sometimes expressed in terms of <i>centistokes</i> (cSt). In U.S. usage, <i>stoke</i> is sometimes used as the singular form.
</p>
<dl><dd>1 St = 1 cm<sup>2</sup>·s<sup>−1</sup> = 10<sup>−4</sup> m<sup>2</sup>·s<sup>−1</sup>.</dd>
<dd>1 cSt = 1 mm<sup>2</sup>·s<sup>−1</sup> = 10<sup>−6 </sup>m<sup>2</sup>·s<sup>−1</sup>.</dd></dl>
<p>Water at 20 °C has a kinematic viscosity of about 10<sup>−6 </sup>m<sup>2</sup>·s<sup>−1</sup> or 1 cSt.
</p><p>The kinematic viscosity is sometimes referred to as <i>diffusivity of momentum</i>, because it is analogous to <a href="/wiki/Thermal_diffusivity" title="Thermal diffusivity">diffusivity of heat</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diffusion_coefficient" title="Diffusion coefficient">diffusivity of mass</a>. It is therefore used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dimensionless_number" title="Dimensionless number">dimensionless numbers</a> which compare the ratio of the diffusivities.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fluidity">Fluidity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Viscosity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Fluidity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Multiplicative_inverse" title="Multiplicative inverse">reciprocal</a> of viscosity is <i>fluidity</i>, usually symbolized by <span class="texhtml"><i>φ</i> = <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>μ</i></span></span></span> or <span class="texhtml"><i>F</i> = <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>μ</i></span></span></span>, depending on the convention used, measured in <i>reciprocal poise</i> (P<sup>−1</sup>, or <a href="/wiki/Centimetre" title="Centimetre">cm</a>·<a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">s</a>·<a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">g</a><sup>−1</sup>), sometimes called the <i>rhe</i>. <i>Fluidity</i> is seldom used in <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a> practice.
</p><p>The concept of fluidity can be used to determine the viscosity of an <a href="/wiki/Ideal_solution" title="Ideal solution">ideal solution</a>. For two components A and B, the fluidity when A and B are mixed is
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F\approx \chi _{\mathrm {A} }F_{\mathrm {A} }+\chi _{\mathrm {B} }F_{\mathrm {B} },}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>F</mi>
<mo>≈<!-- ≈ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>χ<!-- χ --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</msub>
<msub>
<mi>F</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<p>where <span class="texhtml"><i>χ</i><sub>A</sub></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>χ</i><sub>B</sub></span> are the <a href="/wiki/Mole_fraction" title="Mole fraction">mole fractions</a> of components A and B respectively, and <span class="texhtml"><i>μ</i><sub>A</sub></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>μ</i><sub>B</sub></span> are the components' pure viscosities.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-standard_units">Non-standard units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Viscosity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Non-standard units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Reyn" title="Reyn">reyn</a> is a British unit of dynamic viscosity.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Viscosity_index" title="Viscosity index">Viscosity index</a> is a measure for the change of viscosity with temperature. It is used in the automotive industry to characterise lubricating oil.
</p><p>At one time the petroleum industry relied on measuring kinematic viscosity by means of the Saybolt viscometer, and expressing kinematic viscosity in units of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saybolt_universal_second" title="Saybolt universal second">Saybolt universal seconds</a> (SUS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Other abbreviations such as SSU (<i>Saybolt seconds universal</i>) or SUV (<i>Saybolt universal viscosity</i>) are sometimes used. Kinematic viscosity in centistokes can be converted from SUS according to the arithmetic and the reference table provided in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ASTM" title="ASTM">ASTM</a> D 2161.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Molecular_origins">Molecular origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Viscosity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Molecular origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:University_of_Queensland_Pitch_drop_experiment-white_bg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1524" data-file-width="1012" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/University_of_Queensland_Pitch_drop_experiment-white_bg.jpg/170px-University_of_Queensland_Pitch_drop_experiment-white_bg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/University_of_Queensland_Pitch_drop_experiment-white_bg.jpg/255px-University_of_Queensland_Pitch_drop_experiment-white_bg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/University_of_Queensland_Pitch_drop_experiment-white_bg.jpg/340px-University_of_Queensland_Pitch_drop_experiment-white_bg.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:University_of_Queensland_Pitch_drop_experiment-white_bg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pitch_drop_experiment" title="Pitch drop experiment">Pitch</a> has a viscosity approximately 230 billion (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7011229999999999999♠</span>2.3<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>11</sup></span>) times that of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In general, the viscosity of a system depends in detail on how the molecules constituting the system interact. There are no simple but correct expressions for the viscosity of a fluid. The simplest exact expressions are the <a href="/wiki/Green%E2%80%93Kubo_relations" title="Green–Kubo relations">Green–Kubo relations</a> for the linear shear viscosity or the <i>transient time correlation function</i> expressions derived by Evans and Morriss in 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Although these expressions are each exact, calculating the viscosity of a dense fluid using these relations currently requires the use of <a href="/wiki/Molecular_dynamics" title="Molecular dynamics">molecular dynamics</a> computer simulations. On the other hand, much more progress can be made for a dilute gas. Even elementary assumptions about how gas molecules move and interact lead to a basic understanding of the molecular origins of viscosity. More sophisticated treatments can be constructed by systematically coarse-graining the <a href="/wiki/Equations_of_motion" title="Equations of motion">equations of motion</a> of the gas molecules. An example of such a treatment is <a href="/wiki/Chapman%E2%80%93Enskog_theory" title="Chapman–Enskog theory">Chapman–Enskog theory</a>, which derives expressions for the viscosity of a dilute gas from the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_equation" title="Boltzmann equation">Boltzmann equation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1970_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chapman1970-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Momentum transport in gases is generally mediated by discrete molecular collisions, and in liquids by attractive forces which bind molecules close together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bird2007_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bird2007-25">[25]</a></sup> Because of this, the dynamic viscosities of liquids are typically several orders of magnitude higher than dynamic viscosities of gases.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gases">Gases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Viscosity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Gases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_theory_of_gases" title="Kinetic theory of gases">Kinetic theory of gases</a></div>
<p>Viscosity in gases arises principally from the <a href="/wiki/Molecular_diffusion" title="Molecular diffusion">molecular diffusion</a> that transports momentum between layers of flow. An elementary calculation for a dilute gas at temperature <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle T}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

Even without <i>a priori</i> knowledge of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \alpha }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

In particular, since <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \lambda }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mstyle>
</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mu " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9fd47b2a39f7a7856952afec1f1db72c67af6161" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:1.402ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> itself should increase with temperature and be <i>independent</i> of density. In fact, both of these predictions persist in more sophisticated treatments, and accurately describe experimental observations. Note that this behavior runs counter to common intuition regarding liquids, for which viscosity typically <i>decreases</i> with temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bird2007_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bird2007-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bellac2004_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bellac2004-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>

<th>Elementary calculation of viscosity for a dilute gas<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>
<p>Consider a dilute gas moving parallel to the <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>y</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle u(y)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="u(y)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d8dff407583551a7838f4c98386a715c505eda3f" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.294ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> that depends only on the <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle y}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

To simplify the discussion, the gas is assumed to have uniform temperature and density.
</p><p>Under these assumptions, the <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mstyle>
</mrow>

Because <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \lambda }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mu ={\frac {\alpha }{\pi ^{3/2}}}{\frac {\sqrt {k_{B}mT}}{\sigma ^{2}}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \mu ={\frac {\alpha }{\pi ^{3/2}}}{\frac {\sqrt {k_{B}mT}}{\sigma ^{2}}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f8edf7e76a918841b30979be04310f1a64a62669" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:19.543ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>In this case <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \lambda }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \lambda }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\lambda " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b43d0ea3c9c025af1be9128e62a18fa74bedda2a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.355ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is independent of temperature, so <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mu \propto T^{1/2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>μ<!-- μ --></mi>
<mo>∝<!-- ∝ --></mo>
<msup>
<mi>T</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mu \propto T^{1/2}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \mu \propto T^{1/2}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bf4eec5fd44de3461bf9ab6c72a545de66dcd351" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:8.918ex; height:3.343ex;"/></span>. For more complicated molecular models, however, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \lambda }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \lambda }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\lambda " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b43d0ea3c9c025af1be9128e62a18fa74bedda2a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.355ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> depends on temperature in a non-trivial way, and simple kinetic arguments as used here are inadequate. More fundamentally, the notion of a mean free path becomes imprecise for particles that interact over a finite range, which limits the usefulness of the concept for describing real-world gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Chapman.E2.80.93Enskog_theory"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chapman–Enskog_theory">Chapman–Enskog theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Viscosity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Chapman–Enskog theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chapman%E2%80%93Enskog_theory" title="Chapman–Enskog theory">Chapman–Enskog theory</a></div>
<p>A technique developed by <a href="/wiki/Sydney_Chapman_(mathematician)" title="Sydney Chapman (mathematician)">Sydney Chapman</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Enskog" title="David Enskog">David Enskog</a> in the early 1900s allows a more refined calculation of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>μ<!-- μ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mu }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mu " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9fd47b2a39f7a7856952afec1f1db72c67af6161" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:1.402ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1970_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chapman1970-24">[24]</a></sup> It is based on the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_equation" title="Boltzmann equation">Boltzmann equation</a>, which provides a systematic statistical description of a dilute gas in terms of intermolecular interactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> As such, their technique allows accurate calculation of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>μ<!-- μ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mu }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mu " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9fd47b2a39f7a7856952afec1f1db72c67af6161" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:1.402ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> for more realistic molecular models, such as those incorporating intermolecular attraction rather than just hard-core repulsion.
</p><p>It turns out that a more realistic modeling of interactions is essential for accurate prediction of the temperature dependence of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>μ<!-- μ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mu }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mu " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9fd47b2a39f7a7856952afec1f1db72c67af6161" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:1.402ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>, which experiments show increases more rapidly than the <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle T^{1/2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mi>T</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle T^{1/2}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="T^{1/2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/20a2f8e9d493c53592f6eae070aeb0f5ca398a30" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:4.418ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> trend predicted for rigid elastic spheres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bird2007_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bird2007-25">[25]</a></sup> Indeed, the Chapman–Enskog analysis shows that the predicted temperature dependence can be tuned by varying the parameters in various molecular models. A simple example is the Sutherland model,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> which describes rigid elastic spheres with <i>weak</i> mutual attraction. In such a case, the attractive force can be treated <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_theory" title="Perturbation theory">perturbatively</a>, which leads to a particularly simple expression for <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>μ<!-- μ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mu }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mu " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9fd47b2a39f7a7856952afec1f1db72c67af6161" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:1.402ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mu ={\frac {5}{16\sigma ^{2}}}\left({\frac {k_{B}mT}{\pi }}\right)^{1/2}\left(1+{\frac {S}{T}}\right)^{-1},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle S}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

To connect with experiment, it is convenient to rewrite as

<p>where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mu _{0}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</math></span><img alt="\mu _{0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fe2fd9b8decb38a3cd158e7b6c0c6e2d987fefcc" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:2.456ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the viscosity at temperature <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle T_{0}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

If <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>T</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<msub>
<mi>T</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>0</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="T=T_{0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ed837f1758b02ae3912d00544954a950d76be7ca" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:7.147ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> and at least one other temperature, then <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle S}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>S</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="S" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4611d85173cd3b508e67077d4a1252c9c05abca2" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.499ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> can be calculated. It turns out that expressions for <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>μ<!-- μ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mu }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mu " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9fd47b2a39f7a7856952afec1f1db72c67af6161" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:1.402ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> obtained in this way are accurate for a number of gases over a sizable range of temperatures. On the other hand, Chapman and Cowling<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1970_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chapman1970-24">[24]</a></sup> argue that this success does not imply that molecules actually interact according to the Sutherland model. Rather, they interpret the prediction for <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>μ<!-- μ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mu }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mu " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9fd47b2a39f7a7856952afec1f1db72c67af6161" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:1.402ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> as a simple interpolation which is valid for some gases over fixed ranges of temperature, but otherwise does not provide a picture of intermolecular interactions which is fundamentally correct and general. Slightly more sophisticated models, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lennard%E2%80%93Jones_potential" title="Lennard–Jones potential">Lennard–Jones potential</a>, may provide a better picture, but only at the cost of a more opaque dependence on temperature. In some systems the assumption of spherical symmetry must be abandoned as well, as is the case for vapors with highly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_molecules" title="Polar molecules">polar molecules</a> like <a href="/wiki/Properties_of_water" title="Properties of water">H<sub>2</sub>O</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liquids">Liquids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Viscosity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Liquids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>In contrast with gases, there is no simple yet accurate picture for the molecular origins of viscosity in liquids. 
</p><p>At the simplest level of description, the relative motion of adjacent layers in a liquid is opposed primarily by attractive molecular forces
acting across the layer boundary. In this picture, one (correctly) expects viscosity to decrease with temperature. This is because
increasing temperature increases the random thermal motion of the molecules, which makes it easier for them to overcome their attractive interactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reid1958_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reid1958-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Building on this visualization, a simple theory can be constructed in analogy with the discrete structure of a solid: groups of molecules in a liquid 
are visualized as forming "cages" which surround and enclose single molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bird2007_p29-31_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bird2007_p29-31-34">[34]</a></sup> These cages can be occupied or unoccupied, and
stronger molecular attraction corresponds to stronger cages.
Due to random thermal motion, a molecule "hops" between cages at a rate which varies inversely with the strength of molecular attractions. In <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_equilibrium" title="Thermodynamic equilibrium">equilibrium</a> these "hops" are not biased in any direction.
On the other hand, in order for two adjacent layers to move relative to each other, the "hops" must be biased in the direction
of the relative motion. The force required to sustain this directed motion can be estimated for a given shear rate, leading to
</p>
